The Ghurabiyya: Resemblance and Jibril's Mistake
Describes the Ghurabiyya's belief about Jibril's mistake due to resemblance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme sectarian belief of the Ghurabiyya that Jibril mistakenly gave prophethood to Muhammad instead of Ali due to their resemblance.

Core Idea
The Ghurabiyya claimed Gabriel confused Muhammad and Ali — a grave error in angelic infallibility.

This lesson introduces the Ghurabiyya, an extremist sect that held a bizarre view about the resemblance between the Prophet Muhammad and Imam Ali. They believed that the angel Gabriel mistakenly conveyed revelation to the wrong person, which contradicts core Islamic teachings about the infallibility of angels in delivering divine messages.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the short Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the comparison to crows and flies used to illustrate the claimed degree of resemblance.
The passage is brief; read it twice to absorb the claim.
2
Identify the core claim
The Ghurabiyya assert that Gabriel was sent to Ali but mistakenly went to Muhammad. Identify why they think this happened (extreme resemblance).
Contrast this with orthodox Islamic belief in angelic infallibility.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this belief is considered heretical. How does it conflict with the Islamic doctrine that angels do not err in delivering revelation?
This sect is mentioned as a deviant view to be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغرابية قالوا: محمد بعلي أشبه من الغراب بالغراب والذباب بالذباب، فبعث الله جبريل إلى علي فغلط جبريل في تبلغ الرسالة من علي إلى محمد. قال شاعرهم:
The Ghurabiyya said: Muhammad resembles Ali more than a crow resembles a crow, and a fly resembles a fly. So God sent Gabriel to Ali, and Gabriel made a mistake in delivering the message from Ali to Muhammad. Their poet said:
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage describes the Ghurabiyya sect, which held an extreme view that Muhammad and Ali were so physically similar that the angel Gabriel confused them and mistakenly gave prophethood to Muhammad instead of Ali. The name 'Ghurabiyya' is derived from the word 'ghurab' (crow) because of their comparison of their resemblance to that of two crows. The passage then mentions that their poet composed verses (not included in the text). The author presents only the core claim and the beginning of a poetic citation.

Key Terms
الغرابية
الغرب
جبريل
الرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ghurabiyya believed that the angel Gabriel could make a mistake in delivering revelation, confusing Muhammad and Ali.
Correction: Islamic theology holds that angels, especially Gabriel, are infallible in conveying divine messages; they do not err or confuse recipients.

Checkpoint
According to the Ghurabiyya, why did Muhammad receive prophethood instead of Ali?
A) Because Ali refused it
B) Because Gabriel made a mistake due to their resemblance
C) Because Muhammad was more worthy
D) Because Ali was already an imam

Key Takeaway
The Ghurabiyya held the heretical belief that Gabriel mistakenly gave prophethood to Muhammad instead of Ali due to their resemblance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ghurabiyya relied on physical resemblance to form a belief about prophethood. Where in your own life might you be judging something important—like a person's character or the truth of an idea—based on outward appearance rather than the clear evidence of revelation? What step will you take today to correct that?
?
If angels like Gabriel are infallible in delivering Allah's message, what does that mean for your confidence in the Qur'an? When you read a verse that challenges you, do you trust it completely, or do you sometimes think it might have been distorted? Write down one verse you struggle with and commit to accepting it as it is.
?
The Ghurabiyya's error stemmed from an extreme about who deserved prophethood. In your heart, do you ever question that Allah chose Muhammad ﷺ as the final prophet? Have you ever wished that prophethood or leadership went to someone else—maybe someone you admire more? Repent from that thought and affirm that Allah's choice is perfect.

The Janahiyya Sect: Metempsychosis and Antinomianism
Describes Janahiyya's metempsychosis, hidden imam, and antinomianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive doctrines of the Janahiyya sect, including metempsychosis, the hidden imam belief, and their antinomian stance.

Core Idea
The Janahiyya believed the spirit of God moved through prophets and imams, with their leader hidden in a mountain.

This passage introduces one of the ghulat (extremist) Shiite sects, the Janahiyya. Their doctrines illustrate how excessive reverence for the imams led to belief in divine incarnation, metempsychosis, and rejection of Islamic law. Understanding these views helps clarify the boundaries of orthodox Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the claims made by the Janahiyya.
Notice the chain of figures: Adam, Seth, prophets, imams, Ali, his three sons, Abdullah.
2
Analyze the chain of transmigration
Identify the sequence of individuals in whom the 'spirit of God' resided according to the Janahiyya. Reflect on the implications of such a belief for the nature of prophethood and imamate.
3
Examine the antinomian consequences
Note that the Janahiyya denied the resurrection and permitted wine, carrion, and fornication. Consider how such antinomianism often accompanies extreme esoteric beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتناسخ وكان روح الله في آدم ثم في شيث ثم الأنبياء والأئمة حتى انتهت إلى علي وأولاده الثلاثة ثم إلى عبد الله، هذا و قالت الجناحية : ( هو ) عبد الله ( حي ) مقيم بجبل بأصفهان) وسيخرج ) وأنكروا القيامة واستحلوا المحرمات من الخمر والميتة والزنا وغيرها .
They believed in metempsychosis and that the spirit of God was in Adam, then in Seth, then in the prophets and imams until it ended with Ali and his three sons, then to Abdullah. This, and the Janahiyya said: 'He is Abdullah, alive, residing in a mountain in Isfahan, and he will emerge.' And they denied the resurrection and deemed permissible forbidden things like wine, carrion, fornication, and others.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage introduces the belief in tanasukh (metempsychosis) among the ghulat sects, specifically the Janahiyya. The author first describes a chain of spiritual succession from Adam through prophets and imams to Abdullah, indicating that the divine spirit transmigrates through these figures. Then the Janahiyya add that Abdullah is currently alive and residing in a mountain in Isfahan and will appear in the future. They also reject the resurrection and permit major sins, showing their antinomian tendencies.

Key Terms
تناسخ
روح الله
الجناحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Janahiyya's beliefs represent mainstream Shiite theology.
Correction: The Janahiyya are considered ghulat (extremists) and their doctrines, such as metempsychosis and antinomianism, are rejected by both Sunni and mainstream Shiite orthodoxy.

Checkpoint
What did the Janahiyya believe about Abdullah?
A) He was dead and buried
B) He was alive in a mountain in Isfahan and will emerge
C) He was an angel
D) He was a prophet

Key Takeaway
The Janahiyya sect illustrates how extreme reverence for the imams can lead to doctrines of incarnation, metempsychosis, and antinomianism, all of which are rejected by orthodox Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Janahiyya claimed the spirit of God inhabited human beings, do you ever find yourself unconsciously elevating any scholar, leader, or imam beyond their human status? What specific steps can you take today to ensure your reverence is directed solely to Allah?
?
The Janahiyya rejected the resurrection and deemed forbidden things permissible. Is there any area of your life where you rationalize disobedience to clear divine commands? What will you do this week to realign your actions with the Sharia, even if it is difficult?

The Ghurabiyya Sect: Gabriel's Alleged Mistake
The author recounts the Ghurabiyya's belief that Gabriel misdelivered prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme belief of the Ghurabiyya sect that Gabriel mistakenly delivered prophethood to Muhammad instead of Ali, and why this was considered a shocking claim.

Core Idea
The Ghurabiyya believed Gabriel made a mistake—a claim of divine error.

The Ghurabiyya were a small sect that carried their devotion to Ali to an extreme. Their name derives from 'ghurab' (crow), based on a comparison of similarity. This passage presents their core belief, along with a poet's verse that curses Gabriel for his supposed error.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the comparison made and the accusation against Gabriel.
The passage is concise but packs a strong claim.
2
Understand the similarity claim
The Ghurabiyya said Muhammad and Ali were as similar as two crows or two flies. This extreme similarity is the basis for their argument.
3
Identify the allegation against Gabriel
They claimed God sent Gabriel to Ali, but Gabriel mistakenly delivered the message (prophethood) to Muhammad.
This is a direct charge of error against an angel.
4
Examine the poet's verse
The poet calls Gabriel 'the trustworthy one' (al-Amin) but says he erred. The sect curses Gabriel, calling him 'the one with feathers'.
This shows their animosity toward Gabriel.
5
Reflect on the doctrinal boundary
Think about why mainstream Islam considers this belief a major deviation—it questions Gabriel's infallibility and God's plan.
This helps in understanding the limits of acceptable devotion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغرابية قالوا : محمد بعلي أشبه من الغراب بالغراب ) والذباب بالذباب فبعث الله جبريل إلى علي فغلط جبريل) في تبلغ الرسالة ( من علي إلى محمد ) قال شاعرهم : غلط الأمين فجازها عن حيدره فيلعنون صاحب الريش يعنون به جبريل
The Ghurabiyya said: 'Muhammad is more similar to ‘Ali than a crow to a crow, and a fly to a fly, so God sent Gabriel to ‘Ali, but Gabriel made a mistake in delivering the message from ‘Ali to Muhammad.' One of their poets said: 'The trustworthy one [Gabriel] erred and delivered it past Haydar ['Ali].' So they curse the one with feathers, meaning Gabriel.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This short passage describes the Ghurabiyya sect. They claimed that Muhammad and ‘Ali were so similar that God sent Gabriel to give prophethood to ‘Ali, but Gabriel mistakenly gave it to Muhammad. Their poet composed a verse accusing Gabriel of error. Consequently, they curse Gabriel, referring to him as 'the one with feathers'. This shows their extreme ‘Alid loyalty.

Key Terms
الغرابية
جبريل
الغراب
الأمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ghurabiyya simply believed in the superiority of Ali over Muhammad.
Correction: In reality, they claimed that Gabriel made a mistake—a far more severe position that challenges the integrity of divine revelation.

Checkpoint
What did the Ghurabiyya do to Gabriel?
A) They praised him
B) They cursed him
C) They considered him a prophet
D) They ignored him

Key Takeaway
The Ghurabiyya went beyond mere veneration of Ali to accusing the angel Gabriel of a grave mistake, marking a clear boundary in Islamic orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Ghurabiyya cursed Gabriel as 'the one with feathers,' do you sense any resentment in your own heart toward those who delivered Allah's message to you? How might you purify your heart from such hidden grudges?
?
If you truly believed that an angel could make a mistake in revelation, how would that affect your trust in every verse of the Quran? What does this lesson reveal about the importance of affirming angelic infallibility in your daily recitation?
?
The Ghurabiyya elevated their love for Ali to the point of accusing Gabriel of error. Is there any person or cause you love so much that you are willing to distort or overlook truth? What one step can you take today to ensure your loyalties remain subordinate to Allah's word?

The Dhammiyya Sect: Censure of the Prophet and Claims of Divinity
The author presents the Dhammiyya who censure Muhammad and affirm the divinity of five persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core beliefs of the Dhammiyya sect, especially their departure from Islamic teachings regarding prophethood and divinity.

Core Idea
A deviant sect that denigrates Muhammad and elevates Ali and others to divinity.

The Dhammiyya represent an extreme deviation in Islamic thought. They not only rejected the finality of prophethood but also associated divinity with human beings. This passage outlines their beliefs, including the censure of Muhammad and the deification of Ali, sometimes extending to the Prophet himself and the Ahl al-Bayt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the basic claims. Note the key terms: الذمية, ذموا محمداً, أصحاب العباء.
2
Understand the name and origin
The name 'Dhammiyya' comes from their censure (dhamm) of Muhammad. Ask yourself: why would a group that claims to follow Islam revile the Prophet?
They believe Ali is the true god and Muhammad failed to call people to him.
3
Analyze the degrees of deification
The passage presents three gradations: some deify only Ali; some deify Ali and Muhammad (with disagreement over who is greater); some deify five persons — the 'People of the Cloak' (Ahl al-Kisa).
Note their inconsistency and the arbitrary exclusion of Fatima from divinity due to gender bias.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this sect contradicts fundamental Islamic tenets: Tawhid (Oneness of God), the finality of prophethood, and the honor of the Prophet's family.
This highlights the importance of sound theology in protecting faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذمية) لقبوا بذلك لأنهم (ذموا محمداً لأن علياً هو الإله وقد بعثه ليدعو الناس إليه فدعا إلى نفسه وقيل بالهيتهما ) أي قالت طائفة منهم بإلهية محمد وعلي ( ولهم في التقديم خلاف ) فبعضهم يقدم علياً في أحكام الإلهية وبعضهم يقدم محمداً ، وقيل بالهية خمسة أشخاص) يسمون أصحاب العباء (هما) وفاطمة والحسنان ) وهؤلاء زعموا أن هذه الخمسة شيء واحد وأن الروح حالة فيهم بالسوية لا مزية لواحد منهم على آخر ( ولا يقولون فاطمة تحاشياً عن وصمة التأنيث
The Dhammiyya: They were so named because they censured Muhammad, because ‘Ali is the god, and He sent him to call people to Him, but he called to himself. And it was said about their divinity: i.e., a group of them said that Muhammad and ‘Ali are both divine. And they differ over precedence: some give precedence to ‘Ali in the rulings of divinity, and some give precedence to Muhammad. And it was said about the divinity of five persons, called the People of the Cloak: the two [Muhammad and ‘Ali?], Fatima, and al-Hasan and al-Husayn. And these claimed that these five are one thing, and that the spirit dwells in them equally, with no superiority of one over another. And they do not call Fatima [a god] to avoid the stigma of femininity.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
The Dhammiyya sect is named for their censure (dhamm) of Muhammad. They believe that ‘Ali is the true god and that Muhammad was sent to call people to ‘Ali but he called to himself. Some among them extend divinity to Muhammad as well, with debates over precedence. Another view includes five persons: Muhammad, ‘Ali, Fatima, Hasan, and Husayn, claiming they are one entity with the spirit equally distributed. They avoid calling Fatima a god explicitly due to her gender, reflecting their misogyny.

Key Terms
الذمية
ذموا محمداً
أصحاب العباء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Dhammiyya only deified Ali, but the text shows they also deified Muhammad and even five figures.
Correction: The passage explicitly mentions that some said 'Muhammad and Ali are both divine' and 'divinity of five persons'—showing multiple gradations.

Checkpoint
Why are the Dhammiyya so named?
A) Because they praise Muhammad
B) Because they censure Muhammad
C) Because they follow a leader named Dhamma
D) Because they are from the Dhamm tribe

Key Takeaway
The Dhammiyya sect represents an extreme deviation that combines censure of the Prophet with claims of divinity for Ali and his family, contradicting fundamental Islamic monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone today claimed that Prophet Muhammad failed in his mission and should have directed people to someone else, how would you refute them using the core Islamic teachings this passage violates? Be specific: what exactly about their claim contradicts tawhid?
?
The Dhammiyya avoided calling Fatima a god because of their prejudice against femininity. Examine your own heart: are there any assumptions about gender that could distort your understanding of Allah's attributes or the honor of righteous women like Fatima? What step will you take to correct such biases?
?
This sect claimed five persons are 'one thing' with the spirit dwelling equally. How does this contradict the Islamic belief in the oneness of Allah and the finality of prophethood? What is the real-world consequence if such ideas are left unchecked in Muslim communities?

Ibn al-Hakam's Anthropomorphic Description
Ibn al-Hakam details God's corporeal attributes, including shape, senses, movement, and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed anthropomorphic attributes that Hisham ibn al-Hakam ascribed to God, including dimensions, sensory qualities, and movement.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Hakam described God as a radiant, three-dimensional body with sensory qualities and movement.

This lesson focuses on the views of Hisham ibn al-Hakam, one of the two founders of the Hishamiyya sect. He agreed that God is a body but provided a remarkably detailed description involving dimensions, light, and even taste and smell. We'll examine each attribute he proposed and their theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation to grasp the range of attributes Ibn al-Hakam assigns to God.
Note that the passage includes both common ground and Ibn al-Hakam's specifics.
2
Identify the core attributes
List the attributes: shape as a three-dimensional equal ingot, sensory qualities, standing/sitting, similarity to bodies, knowledge via ray, seven spans, touching throne, will as movement, temporal knowledge, speech attribute, accident exclusion, imam infallibility.
These are all mentioned in the passage.
3
Analyze the theological deviations
Compare each attribute with mainstream Islamic Aqeedah (e.g., God is not a body, not limited by space, not subject to change).
This highlights the sect's extreme anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the overall coherence
Consider how the attributes form a consistent picture of God as a physical entity, despite internal inconsistencies.
For instance, God is both a pure ingot and has limbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهشامية : أصحاب الهشامين ابن الحكم وابن سالم الجواليقي ( قالوا : الله جسد ( اتفقوا على ذلك ثم اختلفوا، فقال ابن الحكم هو طويل عريض عميق متساو طوله وعرضه وعمقه ( وهو كالسبيكة البيضاء) الصافية ( يتلألأ من كل جانب وله لون وطعم ورائحة ومجسة)، بفتح الميم هو الموضع الذي يجسه الطبيب كأنهم يريدون بها النبض، قالوا : ( وليست هذه الصفات المذكورة غيره ) أي غير ذاته تعالى ( ويقوم الله ( ويقعد ) ويتحرك ويسكن وله مشابهة بالأجسام لولاها لم تدل عليه ويعلم ما تحت الثرى بشعاع ينفصل عنه إليه وهو سبعة أشبار بأشبار نفسه مماس للعرش بلا تفاوت بينهما أي على وجه لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر ( وإرادته ) تعالى ( حركة هي لا عينه ولا غيره وإنما يعلم الأشياء بعد كونها لا قبله ( بعلم لا قديم ولا حادث ) لأنه صفة والصفة لا توصف ( وكلامه صفة له لا مخلوق ولا غيره ) لما مر . ( والأعراض لا تدل على الباري ) إنما الدال عليه هو الأجسام لما عرفت من مشابهته إياها ( والأئمة معصومون دون الأنبياء ) لأن النبي يوحى إليه فيتقرب به إلى الله بخلاف الإمام فإنه لا إليه فوجب أن يكون يوحى معصوما . ( وقال ابن سالم هو على صورة إنسان له يد ورجل وحواس خمس وأنف وأذن وعين وفم ( وله وفرة سوداء ونصفه الأعلى مجوف ) والأسفل مصمت إلا أنه ليس لحماً ودماً .
The Hishamiyya: The followers of the two Hishams: Ibn al-Hakam and Ibn Salim al-Jawaliqi. They said: 'God is a body.' They agreed on that, then differed. Ibn al-Hakam said: 'He is long, wide, deep, equal in length, width, and depth, and He is like a pure white ingot, shining from every side, and He has color, taste, smell, and palpation (with the opening of the mīm, it is the place that the doctor palpates, as if they mean the pulse).' They said: 'These mentioned attributes are not other than Him, i.e., other than His essence, may He be exalted. And God stands and sits, moves and is still, and He has similarity to bodies; were it not for that, they would not indicate Him. And He knows what is beneath the earth by a ray that separates from Him to it, and He is seven spans by His own span, touching the Throne without difference between them, i.e., in a manner that one does not exceed the other. And His will is a movement that is neither His essence nor other than it, and He only knows things after they exist, not before, with a knowledge that is neither eternal nor originated, because it is an attribute, and an attribute is not described. And His speech is an attribute of His, neither created nor other, as previously. And accidents do not indicate the Creator; rather, what indicates Him is bodies, because of His similarity to them. And the imams are infallible but not the prophets, because the prophet receives revelation so he draws near to God through it, unlike the imam, for he has no revelation, so it is necessary that he be infallible through inspiration.' And Ibn Salim said: 'He is in the form of a human, having a hand, foot, five senses, nose, ear, eye, and mouth, and He has a black forelock, and His upper half is hollow, and the lower solid, except that it is not flesh and blood.'
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage details the anthropomorphism of the Hishamiyya sect. Both Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn Salim agree God is a body, but differ in description. Ibn al-Hakam describes God as a uniform, shining, three-dimensional body with sensory qualities, not separate from His essence. He attributes to God spatial movement, bodily resemblance, and a ray of knowledge. He holds that God's will is a non-essential movement, knowledge is temporal, and speech is an attribute. He also claims that bodies, not accidents, indicate God due to His similarity to them. He asserts imams are infallible through inspiration, unlike prophets. Ibn Salim's view is more explicitly anthropomorphic: God has human form with limbs, senses, a black forelock, and partially hollow anatomy, but not made of flesh and blood.

Key Terms
الله جسد
سبيكة بيضاء
مشابهة بالأجسام
شعاع
إرادته حركة
الأئمة معصومون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Hishamiyya only held a simple belief that God is a body.
Correction: The passage shows they had highly detailed and specific anthropomorphic descriptions, including dimensions, senses, and mechanisms of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Hakam, how does God know things under the earth?
A) Through angels
B) By a ray that separates from Him
C) Through prophets
D) By immediate perception

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Hakam's anthropomorphism is extremely detailed, describing God as a physical body with dimensions, senses, and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever subtly imagine Him with human-like features, such as a form, location, or movement? This passage shows how far such thinking can go. What specific images or limitations do you need to repent from and replace with the Quranic understanding that 'Nothing is like Him'?
?
The Hishamiyya reduced God to a physical being with senses and dimensions. In your daily worship, do you ever treat God as if He is limited by time, space, or human emotions? Name one concrete way you can train your heart to affirm His absolute transcendence.
?
This passage attributes knowledge to God via a physical ray. Have you ever subconsciously thought that God's knowledge works like human knowledge—needing to 'find out' or 'receive' information? How will you actively combat such notions in your mind from now on?

Ibn Salim's Anthropomorphic Description
Ibn Salim describes God as having human form with limbs, senses, and a hollow anatomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hisham ibn Salim described God's form in human-like terms, including specific body parts and hollow anatomy.

Core Idea
Ibn Salim described God as a human-like figure with limbs and organs but not made of flesh.

This lesson examines the views of Hisham ibn Salim, the other founder of the Hishamiyya sect. While agreeing that God is a body, he provided an even more anthropomorphic description, portraying God in human form with specific body parts and features, though not of flesh and blood. We'll analyze his description and its departure from mainstream Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation, paying attention to the specific anatomical features mentioned.
Ibn Salim's description is brief but explicit.
2
List the human attributes
Identify all human-like features: hand, foot, five senses, nose, ear, eye, mouth, black forelock, hollow upper half, solid lower half.
Note the qualifier that it is not flesh and blood.
3
Consider the significance of the hollow upper half
Reflect on why the upper half is described as hollow and the lower as solid, and what this implies about God's anatomy.
This is a unique description not found in other anthropomorphic sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهشامية : أصحاب الهشامين ابن الحكم وابن سالم الجواليقي ( قالوا : الله جسد ( اتفقوا على ذلك ثم اختلفوا، فقال ابن الحكم هو طويل عريض عميق متساو طوله وعرضه وعمقه ( وهو كالسبيكة البيضاء) الصافية ( يتلألأ من كل جانب وله لون وطعم ورائحة ومجسة)، بفتح الميم هو الموضع الذي يجسه الطبيب كأنهم يريدون بها النبض، قالوا : ( وليست هذه الصفات المذكورة غيره ) أي غير ذاته تعالى ( ويقوم الله ( ويقعد ) ويتحرك ويسكن وله مشابهة بالأجسام لولاها لم تدل عليه ويعلم ما تحت الثرى بشعاع ينفصل عنه إليه وهو سبعة أشبار بأشبار نفسه مماس للعرش بلا تفاوت بينهما أي على وجه لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر ( وإرادته ) تعالى ( حركة هي لا عينه ولا غيره وإنما يعلم الأشياء بعد كونها لا قبله ( بعلم لا قديم ولا حادث ) لأنه صفة والصفة لا توصف ( وكلامه صفة له لا مخلوق ولا غيره ) لما مر . ( والأعراض لا تدل على الباري ) إنما الدال عليه هو الأجسام لما عرفت من مشابهته إياها ( والأئمة معصومون دون الأنبياء ) لأن النبي يوحى إليه فيتقرب به إلى الله بخلاف الإمام فإنه لا إليه فوجب أن يكون يوحى معصوما . ( وقال ابن سالم هو على صورة إنسان له يد ورجل وحواس خمس وأنف وأذن وعين وفم ( وله وفرة سوداء ونصفه الأعلى مجوف ) والأسفل مصمت إلا أنه ليس لحماً ودماً .
The Hishamiyya: The followers of the two Hishams: Ibn al-Hakam and Ibn Salim al-Jawaliqi. They said: 'God is a body.' They agreed on that, then differed. Ibn al-Hakam said: 'He is long, wide, deep, equal in length, width, and depth, and He is like a pure white ingot, shining from every side, and He has color, taste, smell, and palpation (with the opening of the mīm, it is the place that the doctor palpates, as if they mean the pulse).' They said: 'These mentioned attributes are not other than Him, i.e., other than His essence, may He be exalted. And God stands and sits, moves and is still, and He has similarity to bodies; were it not for that, they would not indicate Him. And He knows what is beneath the earth by a ray that separates from Him to it, and He is seven spans by His own span, touching the Throne without difference between them, i.e., in a manner that one does not exceed the other. And His will is a movement that is neither His essence nor other than it, and He only knows things after they exist, not before, with a knowledge that is neither eternal nor originated, because it is an attribute, and an attribute is not described. And His speech is an attribute of His, neither created nor other, as previously. And accidents do not indicate the Creator; rather, what indicates Him is bodies, because of His similarity to them. And the imams are infallible but not the prophets, because the prophet receives revelation so he draws near to God through it, unlike the imam, for he has no revelation, so it is necessary that he be infallible through inspiration.' And Ibn Salim said: 'He is in the form of a human, having a hand, foot, five senses, nose, ear, eye, and mouth, and He has a black forelock, and His upper half is hollow, and the lower solid, except that it is not flesh and blood.'
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage details the anthropomorphism of the Hishamiyya sect. Both Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn Salim agree God is a body, but differ in description. Ibn al-Hakam describes God as a uniform, shining, three-dimensional body with sensory qualities, not separate from His essence. He attributes to God spatial movement, bodily resemblance, and a ray of knowledge. He holds that God's will is a non-essential movement, knowledge is temporal, and speech is an attribute. He also claims that bodies, not accidents, indicate God due to His similarity to them. He asserts imams are infallible through inspiration, unlike prophets. Ibn Salim's view is more explicitly anthropomorphic: God has human form with limbs, senses, a black forelock, and partially hollow anatomy, but not made of flesh and blood.

Key Terms
على صورة إنسان
يد
رجل
حواس خمس
وفرة سوداء
مجوف
مصمت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Salim believed God is a physical human with flesh and blood.
Correction: He explicitly says God is not flesh and blood, so the form is metaphorical or of a different substance.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Salim, what is the composition of God's body?
A) It is made of flesh and blood like humans
B) It is not flesh and blood
C) It is made of light
D) It is made of spirit

Key Takeaway
Ibn Salim's description of God as a human-like body with hollow upper half is one of the most extreme anthropomorphic views in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you ever inadvertently picture a form or location, even if you intellectually deny it? How does this passage expose the subtle temptations of anthropomorphism in your own heart?
?
If a fellow Muslim today described God as having a black forelock or a body, how would you lovingly correct them based on the teachings of tawhid? Are you prepared to defend God's incomparability?
?
Ibn Salim's description still denies that God is flesh and blood, yet it remains a gross violation of divine transcendence. Reflect: Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, while not as extreme, still fall short of affirming His absolute uniqueness?

The Zurariyya Sect: Created Attributes of God
The author states the Zurariyya's belief that God's attributes are created in time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Zurariyya sect's claim that God's attributes are originated in time and the theological implications of that view.

Core Idea
The Zurariyya believed God's attributes were created, implying He was imperfect before.

This lesson introduces the Zurariyya, a sect that took an extreme position on divine attributes. By claiming that attributes are created in time, they inadvertently denied God's eternal perfection. Let's examine their belief as recorded by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style and the logical chain: sect founder → belief → implication.
The parentheses in the Arabic are from the original text.
2
Identify the key claim about attributes
Find the phrase 'بحدوث الصفات' (origination of attributes). Understand that the Zurariyya affirm attributes but believe they come into existence after not existing.
This is different from denying attributes outright.
3
Reflect on the theological problem
Consider why this belief is problematic: if God lacked attributes before they were created, He would not be perfect. Mainstream Islam holds that God's attributes are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزرارية هو زرارة بن أعين قالوا بحدوث الصفات ) لله ( وقبلها ) أي قبل حدوثها له ( لا حياة ) فلا يكون حينئذ حياً ولا عالماً ولا قادراً ولا سميعاً ولا بصيراً.
The Zurariyya: He is Zurara ibn A‘yan. They said that the attributes are originated for God, and before their origination He had no life, so at that time He was not living, nor knowing, nor powerful, nor hearing, nor seeing.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This short passage describes the Zurariyya sect. They hold that God's attributes (such as life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight) are originated in time. Before they were created, God lacked these attributes, implying that He was not alive or knowledgeable etc. at that time. This is a form of attribute temporality that the author records as a deviant belief.

Key Terms
الزرارية
زرارة بن أعين
حدوث الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Zurariyya denied God's attributes entirely.
Correction: They affirmed attributes but believed they were created in time, so before creation God lacked them.

Checkpoint
What did the Zurariyya believe about God's attributes?
A) They are eternal
B) They are originated after not existing
C) They are identical to His essence
D) They are metaphors

Key Takeaway
The Zurariyya sect held that God's attributes are created in time, which implies that God was imperfect before their creation — a view rejected by mainstream Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult trial, do you ever feel that God is not 'seeing' or 'hearing' because He hasn't responded yet? How does believing in eternal, uncreated attributes change how you wait for His help? If you catch yourself feeling that God's knowledge is incomplete or that He 'hasn't noticed yet,' you have unknowingly embraced the logic of the Zurariyya. What will you do the next time impatience whispers that God is not aware?
?
Are there moments in your life when you act as if God's power is limited—like He cannot guide someone because they are too far, or He cannot forgive you because your sin is too great? The Zurariyya said God's power came later. How would consciously affirming that God's power is eternal and not subject to time change the way you think about your impossible situations? Cite one specific situation today where you will remind yourself that God's power never had a beginning.
?
The Zurariyya believed God lacked hearing before it originated. When you pray, do you ever doubt that God is listening—perhaps because He has not answered yet? What does it reveal about your patience if your certainty in God's hearing depends on getting a quick answer? Write down one prayer where you will trust that God is hearing you right now, even if the answer seems delayed.

The Yunusiyya Sect: God Carried by Angels
The author recounts the Yunusiyya's anthropomorphic belief about God and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Yunusiyya sect's anthropomorphic belief that God is carried by angels and how it deviates from Islamic doctrine.

Core Idea
The Yunusiyya imagined God physically carried by angels, reducing Him to a throne-like object.

Among the early deviant sects that emerged in Islam, the Yunusiyya held a particularly crude anthropomorphic view of God. They claimed that God sits on the Throne and is physically carried by angels, comparing Him to a throne carried by its legs. This lesson examines their belief and its theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through before you interpret.
The passage is short but dense with anthropomorphic imagery.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that God is on the Throne and angels carry Him. Note the founder's name: Yunus ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Qummi.
3
Understand the analogy
The sect compares God to a throne carried by its legs, claiming God is stronger than the angels even though they carry Him. This analogy is used to defend a physical interpretation.
This analogy reveals their crude anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the deviation
Ask yourself why this belief contradicts Islamic teachings on God's transcendence (tanzih). The commentary will help you see why this is a deviant view.
Mainstream Islam affirms God is above the Throne without physical contact or need of support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليونسية : هو يونس بن عبد الرحمن القمي قال : الله تعالى على العرش تحمله الملائكة وهو أقوى منها أي من الملائكة من كونه محمولاً لهم ( كالكرسي يحمله رجلاه ) وهو أقوى منهما .
The Yunusiyya: He is Yunus ibn ‘Abd al-Rahman al-Qummi. He said: 'God Almighty is on the Throne, the angels carry Him, and He is stronger than them – i.e., than the angels, due to His being carried by them – like a throne carried by its legs, and He is stronger than them.'
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage describes the Yunusiyya sect. They believe that God is physically located on the Throne (al-‘arsh) and that angels carry Him. They compare this to a throne carried by its legs, asserting that God is stronger than the angels despite being carried by them. This is a crude anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
اليونسية
يونس بن عبد الرحمن
العرش
تحمله الملائكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Yunusiyya merely affirmed that God is on the Throne in a metaphorical sense.
Correction: In fact, they took it literally, claiming angels physically carry God, which is anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the Yunusiyya, what do the angels do for God?
A) They worship Him
B) They carry Him
C) They guard Him
D) They serve as messengers

Key Takeaway
The Yunusiyya sect's belief that angels carry God is a form of anthropomorphism rejected by orthodox Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's relationship to the Throne, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as needing some kind of support or location? How does the Yunusiyya's explicit anthropomorphism reveal the danger of even subtle mental images? Ask Allah to purify your conception of His transcendence.
?
The Yunusiyya used an analogy to make God's power understandable, but it led to error. Where in your own life do you rely on human analogies to understand God, risking reducing Him to creation? What step will you take today to submit to what He has revealed about Himself without overstepping?
?
If you truly believed God was carried by angels, how would that affect your trust in His absolute power to handle your burdens? Now that you know the correct belief, what burden will you consciously entrust to Him today, knowing He needs no support?

The Shaytaniyya Sect: God as Non-Corporeal Light with Human Form
The author presents the Shaytaniyya's view of God as non-corporeal light yet humanlike.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive beliefs of the Shaytaniyya sect regarding God's nature, knowledge, and imamate.

Core Idea
The Shaytaniyya combined a non-corporeal God with a human form and temporal knowledge.

The Shaytaniyya were a small sect within early Shi‘ism with peculiar theological views. Their founder, Muhammad al-Nu‘man, held that God is a non-corporeal light yet resembles a human, and that His knowledge is temporal. This lesson examines these claims and their implications for Islamic orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Meet the sect and founder
Read who the Shaytaniyya are: followers of Muhammad ibn al-Nu‘man, nicknamed Shaytan al-Taq. Understand the context of early Shi‘ite sects.
Shaytan al-Taq was a nickname, not a literal devil.
2
Analyze the first belief: God's nature
The text says God is 'a non-corporeal light' yet 'in the form of a human'. Notice the apparent contradiction: how can something non-corporeal have a human form? This is a key point of criticism from orthodox theologians.
3
Examine the second belief: God's knowledge
They believed God only knows things after they occur. This contradicts the orthodox view of God's eternal and all-encompassing knowledge.
4
Consider the third belief: imamate
They followed a Kaysanite line of imamate: after ‘Ali, the imam is Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, then his son. This differs from both Twelver and Zaydi positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيطانية : هو محمد بن النعمان الملقب بشيطان الطاق قال : إنه تعالى ( نور غير جسماني) ومع ذلك هو ( على صورة إنسان وإنما يعلم الأشياء بعد كونها إلزامية قالوا : الإمامة) بعد علي (لمحمد بن الحنفية ثم ابنه
The Shaytaniyya: He is Muhammad ibn al-Nu‘man, nicknamed Shaytan al-Taq. He said: 'He, exalted, is a non-corporeal light, yet He is in the form of a human, and He only knows things after they occur, as a necessity.' They said: 'The imamate after ‘Ali belongs to Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, then to his son...'
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage introduces the Shaytaniyya sect. Their leader Muhammad al-Nu‘man held that God is a non-corporeal light but also has a human form. They claim God's knowledge is temporal. They also follow the Kaysanite belief that the imamate passed to Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya after ‘Ali. The text is incomplete but conveys their main ideas.

Key Terms
الشيطانية
شيطان الطاق
نور غير جسماني
صورة إنسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that because they said God is non-corporeal, they denied any form.
Correction: In fact, they affirmed a human form for God, making their position contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the Shaytaniyya, when does God know things?
A) Before they occur
B) After they occur
C) Both before and after
D) He does not know particulars

Key Takeaway
The Shaytaniyya held a self-contradictory view of God as both non-corporeal light and human-like, along with a temporal concept of divine knowledge and a Kaysanite imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shaytaniyya claimed God is both non-corporeal light and in human form—a clear contradiction. Are there contradictions in how you describe God’s attributes? What step will you take today to ensure your understanding of Allah is consistent with the Qur’an and Sunnah?
?
They reduced God's knowledge to only knowing events after they happen. Do you sometimes act as if Allah doesn’t see your secret sins until after you commit them? How will remembering Allah’s eternal, all-encompassing knowledge change your next action or thought?
?
The founder was nicknamed 'Shaytan al-Taq' for his sharpness, yet his followers adopted a distorted creed. Who do you trust for your religious knowledge, and on what basis? Are you willing to question any blind allegiance you may hold?

The Dhimmīyya Sect on Divinity
Describes the beliefs of the Dhimmīyya sect regarding divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core doctrines of the Dhimmīyya sect, including their deification of Ali, Muhammad, and the five people of the cloak.

Core Idea
They claimed divinity resided in human beings, not solely in Allah.

The Dhimmīyya are one of the extremist ghulāt sects that emerged in early Islamic history, alleging that divinity incarnated in specific human figures. This passage outlines their key beliefs, from cursing the angel Jibril to deifying Ali, Muhammad, and even the family of the Prophet. Understanding these extreme views clarifies the boundaries of orthodox Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence of beliefs: cursing Jibril, naming, then multiple versions of divinity.
The passage is dense with multiple claims; read slowly.
2
Identify the sect's name origin
The name 'Dhimmīyya' comes from their slander of Muhammad. Understand why they are called that.
3
Trace the versions of deification
The passage presents three escalating beliefs: (1) Ali alone is divine, (2) both Ali and Muhammad are divine, (3) five persons are divine. Note the disagreement on precedence.
This shows the sect's internal diversity.
4
Reflect on the theological boundary
Consider why these beliefs are considered extreme. The core issue is incarnation (hulūl) and association of partners with Allah.
This contrasts with orthodox tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيلعنون صاحب الريش يعنون به جبريل (الذمية) لقبوا بذلك لأنهم (ذموا محمداً لأن علياً هو الإله وقد بعثه ليدعو الناس إليه فدعا إلى نفسه وقيل بالهيتهما ) أي قالت طائفة منهم بإلهية محمد وعلي ( ولهم في التقديم خلاف ) فبعضهم يقدم علياً في أحكام الإلهية وبعضهم يقدم محمداً ، وقيل بالهية خمسة أشخاص) يسمون أصحاب العباء (هما) وفاطمة والحسنان ) وهؤلاء زعموا أن هذه الخمسة شيء واحد وأن الروح حالة فيهم بالسوية لا مزية لواحد منهم على آخر ( ولا يقولون فاطمة تحاشياً عن وصمة التأنيث
They curse the owner of the feather meaning Jibril. (The Dhimmīyya) were named thus because they slandered Muhammad, saying that Ali is the god and He sent him to call people to Him, so he called to himself. And it was said that there is divinity of both, meaning a faction said Muhammad and Ali are divine. And they disagree over precedence: some give precedence to Ali in rulings of divinity, some give precedence to Muhammad. And it was said that there is divinity of five persons, called the people of the cloak: (the two) and Fatima and al-Hasan and al-Husayn. And these claimed that these five are one thing and the spirit is equally present in them, no one having superiority over another. And they do not say Fatima, avoiding the blemish of femininity.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage defines the Dhimmīyya sect, a group of extremists (ghulāt) who believe in the incarnation of divinity in human figures. They curse Jibril for delivering revelation to Muhammad instead of Ali. Their name derives from their slander of Muhammad. They hold that Ali and Muhammad are divine, and some extend divinity to five persons: Muhammad, Ali, Fatima, Hasan, and Husayn, claiming they are one entity with equal spirit. They exclude Fatima from mention to avoid feminine stigma.

Key Terms
الذمية
صاحب الريش
الإلهية
أصحاب العباء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Dhimmīyya merely exaggerated the virtues of Ali (like other Shia groups).
Correction: They actually asserted literal divinity in human beings, a major departure from tawhid.

Checkpoint
What is the reason the Dhimmīyya curse Jibril?
A) Jibril refused to bow to Adam.
B) Jibril delivered revelation to Muhammad instead of Ali.
C) Jibril is considered a false prophet.
D) Jibril is blamed for the delay in revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Dhimmīyya sect deified human figures, illustrating the theological boundary between tawhid and incarnationist extremism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly wish that divinity could be found in a human being you admire, making it easier to worship? How does this passage expose the danger of that desire and what will you do to guard your heart against it?
?
This sect slandered Jibril for delivering revelation to the wrong person. Are you ever resentful at how Allah chooses whom to honor? How can you train your heart to accept Allah's choices without question?
?
They avoided calling Fatima divine because of femininity. Are you allowing cultural biases to shape your understanding of Allah's attributes? What practical step will you take to ensure your conception of Allah is free from human prejudices?

The Hishāmiyya Doctrine of God as a Body
Describes Ibn al-Hakam's view of God's body with attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hishāmiyya sect's belief that God is a body, as expressed by Hishām ibn al-Hakam, including His description as a luminous ingot with sensible qualities and His similarity to created bodies.

Core Idea
The Hishāmiyya believed God is a physical body with color, taste, and smell.

The Hishāmiyya sect, following Hishām ibn al-Hakam and Hishām ibn Sālim, affirmed that God is a body. However, they differed on the nature of this body. This lesson focuses on Ibn al-Hakam's detailed description, which depicts God as a luminous, uniform substance with human-like attributes. We will examine how they justified this belief and what it implies about God's relationship with creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sect and core belief
The passage names the Hishāmiyya as followers of the two Hishāms and states their agreement that God is a body. Note the initial consensus.
The word 'jasad' (body) is central.
2
Examine Ibn al-Hakam's description
Read how he describes God as having uniform dimensions (equal length, width, depth), being like a white ingot, and possessing color, taste, smell, and a pulse.
Thr pulse metaphor suggests a living body.
3
Understand the identity of attributes
The text states that these attributes are not other than His essence. Consider the theological implications of this identity.
This is a form of monism regarding attributes.
4
Analyze the similarity to bodies
Note the claim that God stands, sits, moves, and rests, and that similarity to bodies is necessary for them to indicate Him. This is an epistemological argument.
Without similarity, bodies would not point to God.
5
Consider God's knowledge and size
Read how God knows under the earth by a ray, and that He is seven spans and touches the Throne. Reflect on the literal anthropomorphism.
The ray and span are distinctive details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهشامية : أصحاب الهشامين ابن الحكم وابن سالم الجواليقي ( قالوا : الله جسد ( اتفقوا على ذلك ثم اختلفوا، فقال ابن الحكم هو طويل عريض عميق متساو طوله وعرضه وعمقه ( وهو كالسبيكة البيضاء) الصافية ( يتلألأ من كل جانب وله لون وطعم ورائحة ومجسة)، بفتح الميم هو الموضع الذي يجسه الطبيب كأنهم يريدون بها النبض، قالوا : ( وليست هذه الصفات المذكورة غيره ) أي غير ذاته تعالى ( ويقوم الله ( ويقعد ) ويتحرك ويسكن وله مشابهة بالأجسام لولاها لم تدل عليه ويعلم ما تحت الثرى بشعاع ينفصل عنه إليه وهو سبعة أشبار بأشبار نفسه مماس للعرش بلا تفاوت بينهما أي على وجه لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر ( وإرادته ) تعالى ( حركة هي لا عينه ولا غيره وإنما يعلم الأشياء بعد كونها لا قبله ( بعلم لا قديم ولا حادث ) لأنه صفة والصفة لا توصف ( وكلامه صفة له لا مخلوق ولا غيره ) لما مر . ( والأعراض لا تدل على الباري ) إنما الدال عليه هو الأجسام لما عرفت من مشابهته إياها ( والأئمة معصومون دون الأنبياء ) لأن النبي يوحى إليه فيتقرب به إلى الله بخلاف الإمام فإنه لا إليه فوجب أن يكون يوحى معصوما . ( وقال ابن سالم هو على صورة إنسان له يد ورجل وحواس خمس وأنف وأذن وعين وفم ( وله وفرة سوداء ونصفه الأعلى مجوف ) والأسفل مصمت إلا أنه ليس لحماً ودماً .
The Hishāmiyya: followers of the two Hishāms: Ibn al-Hakam and Ibn Sālim al-Jawālīqī. They said: God is a body. They agreed on that but then differed. Ibn al-Hakam said: He is long, wide, deep, equal in length, width, and depth, and He is like a white pure ingot gleaming from every side, having color, taste, smell, and a pulse point (with the meem vowel, the place the doctor feels, as if they mean the pulse). They said: These mentioned attributes are not other than Him, i.e., other than His essence. And God stands and sits, moves and rests, and has similarity to bodies; if not for that, they would not indicate Him. And He knows what is under the earth by a ray that separates from Him to it, and He is seven spans by His own span, touching the Throne without difference between them, i.e., in a manner that neither exceeds the other. And His will is a movement that is neither His essence nor other. And He only knows things after they exist, not before, with a knowledge that is neither eternal nor temporal, because it is an attribute and the attribute is not described. And His speech is an attribute of His, neither created nor other, as has passed. And accidents do not indicate the Creator; only bodies indicate Him because of His similarity to them. And the imams are infallible without the prophets, because the prophet receives revelation and thereby draws near to God, unlike the imam who does not, so it is necessary that the imam be infallible. And Ibn Sālim said: He is in the form of a human, having hand, foot, five senses, nose, ear, eye, and mouth, and He has black hair, and His upper half is hollow and the lower solid, except that He is not flesh and blood.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage describes the Hishāmiyya sect, followers of Hishām ibn al-Hakam and Hishām ibn Sālim. They all affirm that God is a body, but differ on its nature. Ibn al-Hakam describes God as a luminous, uniform body with sensible qualities like color, taste, and smell, and even a pulse. He claims that God's similarity to bodies is necessary for our knowledge of Him. He also holds that God knows things by a ray, that He is seven spans, touches the Throne, and that His will is a movement. Knowledge only occurs after events, and it is described as neither eternal nor temporal. Ibn Sālim specifies God is in human form with all features but not flesh and blood. They also believe imams are infallible without prophets.

Key Terms
الهشامية
ابن الحكم
جسد
سبيكة
شعاع
أشبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Hishāmiyya denied God's transcendence outright, but they affirmed similarity to bodies for epistemological reasons, not as a denial of transcendence.
Correction: The passage shows they believed God's similarity to bodies is necessary for bodies to point to Him, implying a form of anthropomorphism rather than pure denial of transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Hakam, how does God know what is under the earth?
A) Through a ray that separates from Him.
B) Through direct vision.
C) Through angels.
D) Through revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Hakam held that God is a physical body with human-like attributes, and that this similarity is necessary for human knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having a shape or form? How does the Hishāmiyya's explicit anthropomorphism challenge you to purify your concept of Allah according to the Quran and Sunnah?
?
Ibn al-Hakam argued that God must resemble bodies for us to know Him. Have you ever felt that you need to imagine a 'thing' to believe in it? Where does that assumption come from, and how can you train yourself to submit to Allah without needing a mental image?
?
This passage describes God as having a pulse and moving. Does your heart feel any discomfort? Why? Use that discomfort to reflect on tanzih (transcendence) and how the Salaf affirmed attributes without takyif (asking how). What one step can you take today to guard your heart against limiting Allah?

The Hishāmiyya on Divine Attributes and Infallibility
Describes Ibn al-Hakam's views on will, knowledge, speech, accidents, imams, and Ibn Sālim's human-form description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique doctrines of the Hishāmiyya regarding God's will, knowledge, speech, and the infallibility of imams, as well as Ibn Sālim's view of God's human form.

Core Idea
The Hishāmiyya held that God's will is a movement and that imams are infallible without prophets.

In addition to affirming God's corporeality, the Hishāmiyya developed specific teachings about God's attributes and the role of imams. Ibn al-Hakam argued that God's will is a movement distinct from His essence, and that His knowledge is not eternal. Hishām ibn Sālim described God as having a human form. This lesson explores these distinctive positions.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Hakam on divine attributes
The passage covers will as a movement, knowledge as non-eternal, and speech as an uncreated attribute. Note each definition.
These are non-standard theological positions.
2
Understand the argument on accidents
Ibn al-Hakam asserts that accidents do not indicate God; only bodies do, due to God's similarity to them. This supports his anthropomorphism.
This contrasts with classical kalam.
3
Consider the infallibility of imams
The text claims imams are infallible without prophets, because prophets receive revelation while imams do not.
This reverses the usual hierarchy.
4
Examine Ibn Sālim's description
Ibn Sālim describes God as having a human form with all external features, but not flesh and blood, and with a hollow upper half.
This is a distinct anthropomorphic view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Hishāmiyya: followers of the two Hishāms: Ibn al-Hakam and Ibn Sālim al-Jawālīqī. They said: God is a body. They agreed on that but then differed. Ibn al-Hakam said: He is long, wide, deep, equal in length, width, and depth, and He is like a white pure ingot gleaming from every side, having color, taste, smell, and a pulse point (with the meem vowel, the place the doctor feels, as if they mean the pulse). They said: These mentioned attributes are not other than Him, i.e., other than His essence. And God stands and sits, moves and rests, and has similarity to bodies; if not for that, they would not indicate Him. And He knows what is under the earth by a ray that separates from Him to it, and He is seven spans by His own span, touching the Throne without difference between them, i.e., in a manner that neither exceeds the other. And His will is a movement that is neither His essence nor other. And He only knows things after they exist, not before, with a knowledge that is neither eternal nor temporal, because it is an attribute and the attribute is not described. And His speech is an attribute of His, neither created nor other, as has passed. And accidents do not indicate the Creator; only bodies indicate Him because of His similarity to them. ?

?

?
If God's knowledge only occurs after events (as Ibn al-Hakam claimed), how would that affect your trust in His plans for your future? Do you truly believe He knows everything before it happens, and if so, how must that change your anxiety about tomorrow?

The Shayṭāniyya Sect: God’s Nature and Imamate
Describes the Shayṭāniyya sect's beliefs on God's nature and imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive beliefs of the Shayṭāniyya sect regarding God's nature as non-corporeal light in human form, temporal knowledge, imamate succession, and incarnation.

Core Idea
The Shayṭāniyya believed God is a non-corporeal light in human form and divinity incarnates in human imams.

This passage introduces the Shayṭāniyya, a sect founded by Muḥammad ibn al-Nu'mān al-Shayṭān al-Ṭāq, known for its radical theological and imamate doctrines. The text covers their views on God’s nature, knowledge, a specific line of imamate, and claims of incarnation and antinomianism. Understanding these ideas illustrates the spectrum of beliefs that early Islamic theology encountered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together to grasp the full description of the Shayṭāniyya sect.
Notice the concise yet packed style of the source.
2
Identify the view of God
The sect describes God as a non-corporeal light but in human form, and holds that He knows things only after they occur. Note the contradiction they assert.
3
Trace the imamate succession
List the line of imams they upheld: after Ali, it goes to Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, then his son, then to the Abbasids, then to al-Mansur.
This line skips the Hasan and Husayn lines.
4
Note the incarnation and practices
They claimed divinity incarnated in Abu Muslim and some in al-Muqanna', and they permitted forbidden acts and neglected obligations.
This shows an extreme antinomian tendency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيطانية : هو محمد بن النعمان الملقب بشيطان الطاق قال : إنه تعالى ( نور غير جسماني) ومع ذلك هو ( على صورة إنسان وإنما يعلم الأشياء بعد كونها إلزامية قالوا : الإمامة) بعد علي (لمحمد بن الحنفية ثم ابنه عبدالله ثم علي بن عبد الله بن عباس ثم أولاده إلى المنصور ثم حل الإله في أبي مسلم وإنه لم يقتل واستحلوا المحارم وتركوا الفرائض ومنهم من ادعى الإلهية في المقنع.
The Shayṭāniyya: it is Muḥammad ibn al-Nu'mān, nicknamed Shayṭān al-Ṭāq. He said: He Most High is a non-corporeal light, and despite that He is in the form of a human, and He only knows things after they occur. They said: The imamate after Ali is for Muḥammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, then his son 'Abdullāh, then 'Alī ibn 'Abdullāh ibn 'Abbās, then his descendants until al-Manṣūr, then divinity incarnated in Abū Muslim, and he was not killed, and they made lawful forbidden things and abandoned obligations, and among them some claimed divinity for al-Muqanna'.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage describes the Shayṭāniyya sect, founded by Muḥammad ibn al-Nu'mān al-Shayṭān al-Ṭāq. They believe God is a non-corporeal light yet appears in human form, and His knowledge is temporal. They also assert a specific line of imamate starting from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, passing through descendants, and then the incarnation of divinity in Abū Muslim, who they believe is not dead. They also mention a group claiming divinity for al-Muqanna'. This sect abandons religious duties and allows forbidden things.

Key Terms
الشيطانية
شيطان الطاق
نور غير جسماني
صورة إنسان
الإمامة
محمد بن الحنفية
أبو مسلم
المقنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shayṭāniyya did not believe in incarnation or anthropomorphism.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they believe God is in human form and that divinity incarnated in Abu Muslim and al-Muqanna'.

Checkpoint
According to the Shayṭāniyya, who did the imamate go to after Ali?
A) Hasan
B) Husayn
C) Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya
D) Abu Muslim

Key Takeaway
The Shayṭāniyya combined anthropomorphism, temporal divine knowledge, and incarnation in their imamate doctrine, leading to antinomian practices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own understanding of God's nature. Are there any subtle ways you might be reducing Him to something physical or limiting His knowledge, even if you don't say it aloud? Ask yourself: Would your prayers change if you believed God only knows after events happen?
?
Is there any person—a scholar, leader, or even a friend—to whom you have attached more authority or reverence than is due? Have you ever excused their faults or followed them uncritically, as if they were infallible? How can you root out this tendency before it leads you away from true tawḥīd?
?
The Shayṭāniyya used their beliefs to abandon obligations and make forbidden things lawful. Examine your own life: are there any Islamic duties you are neglecting or rationalizing away because of a flawed belief about God or religion? What step will you take today to return to the sunna and reject such antinomian thinking?

The Mufawwiḍa Sect on Creation Delegation
Describes the Mufawwiḍa sect's belief that God delegated creation to Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mufawwiḍa sect's belief about creation delegation and how it differs from orthodox Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Some claimed God delegated creation to Muhammad or Ali, making them creators.

We encounter a minor sect called the Mufawwiḍa, who held a radical view about creation. They did not deny God's ultimate power, but they claimed God transferred the act of creating the world to a human being. This lesson explains their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two variants mentioned.
2
Identify the two variants
The passage first states that God delegated creation to Muhammad, then mentions a variant saying it was delegated to Ali. Identify both.
3
Reflect on the theological issue
Think about why this belief is problematic from an orthodox perspective. Who alone is the Creator in Islam?
This connects to the core idea that creation is exclusively God's act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفوضة : قالوا الله فوض خلق الدنيا إلى محمد ) أي الله خلق محمداً وفوض إليه خلق الدنيا فهو الخلاق لها بما فيها وقيل : فوض ذلك إلى علي.
The Mufawwiḍa: they said God delegated the creation of the world to Muhammad, meaning God created Muhammad and delegated to him the creation of the world, so he is the creator of it and what it contains. And it was said: He delegated that to Ali.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage defines the Mufawwiḍa sect, who hold that God created Muhammad and then handed over the creation of the world to him, making Muhammad the actual creator of all things. Some among them say this delegation was given to Ali instead.

Key Terms
المفوضة
فوض
خلق الدنيا
محمد
علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mufawwiḍa simply honor Muhammad or Ali highly, but they actually claim that they are creators.
Correction: The Mufawwiḍa explicitly state that Muhammad (or Ali) is the creator of the world, which is a direct violation of tawḥīd al-rubūbiyya (oneness of Lordship).

Checkpoint
What does the Mufawwiḍa sect believe about the creation of the world?
A) God created it directly.
B) Muhammad created it.
C) Ali created it.
D) Both B and C depending on subgroup.

Key Takeaway
The Mufawwiḍa sect held that God delegated creation to a human being, a view rejected by mainstream Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself trusting more in a person's ability to change your circumstances than in Allah's ultimate control? What does this lesson reveal about where your heart truly places its reliance?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah creates and sustains, why do you sometimes fear or hope in the creation more than the Creator? What concrete step can you take today to redirect your trust solely to Allah?
?
The Mufawwiḍa claimed to honor Muhammad by making him a creator. In your own life, where might you be 'honoring' someone in a way that diminishes the unique glory of Allah? How can you repent from that?

The Budā'iyya Sect and the Concept of Budā'
Describes the Budā'iyya sect's belief that God can change His mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Budā'iyya sect's concept of budā' and why it contradicts the Islamic doctrine of God's omniscience.

Core Idea
Budā' doctrine claims God learns new things, denying His omniscience.

The Budā'iyya sect held a unique and controversial view about God's knowledge. They believed that God could have a change of mind after willing something, implying that He was unaware of future outcomes. This passage introduces their belief and highlights its theological problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'Budā'iyya'
The name comes from 'budā'', meaning the appearance of a new opinion. This sect is named for their belief that God can change His mind.
2
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the precise wording: they allowed that God wills something, then it appears to Him (yabdū lahu).
This contrasts with the Sunni view of God's eternal knowledge.
3
Identify the implication
The passage states that this belief necessitates that God is not knower of outcomes. Recognize why this is problematic for God's omniscience.
If God learns new things, His knowledge is not eternal and complete.
4
Reflect on the theological conflict
Consider why traditional Islamic theology rejects budā'. How does this affect other attributes like God's will and power?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البدائية : جوزوا البدء على الله تعالى أي جوزوا أن يريد الله شيئاً ثم يبدو له أي يظهر عليه ما لم يكن ظاهراً له ويلزمه أن لا يكون الرب عالماً بعواقب الأمور
The Budā'iyya: they allowed budā' for God Most High, i.e., they allowed that God wills something, then it appears to Him (yabdū lahu) meaning something becomes manifest to Him that was not manifest to Him, and it necessitates that the Lord is not knower of the outcomes of matters.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage describes the Budā'iyya sect, who claim that God can have a change of mind or new awareness after willing. They permit the concept of budā' (appearance of new knowledge), implying that God is not omniscient of future consequences, which contradicts orthodox belief in God's eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
البدائية
البدء
عواقب الأمور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think budā' is a metaphorical expression for God's actions.
Correction: The Budā'iyya take it literally, meaning God actually becomes aware of new information, which contradicts His omniscience.

Checkpoint
What does the Budā'iyya sect allow for God?
A) Incarnation
B) Corporeality
C) Budā' (change of mind)
D) Eternal attributes

Key Takeaway
The Budā'iyya belief in budā' is incompatible with divine omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Budā'iyya believed God learns new things. When you worry about the future, are you not implicitly acting as if God does not already know what will happen? How does this lesson expose that subtle lack of trust in His omniscience, and what specific step can you take today to replace worry with reliance on His complete knowledge?
?
If someone told you that God can change His mind based on your actions, how would you respond using the reasoning from this passage? Is there any area of your life where you assume God's plan is uncertain and needs adjustment? What does that reveal about your heart?

The Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya: Incarnation of God in Ali
Describes the Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya sects' belief that God incarnated in Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya sects' doctrine that God incarnated in Ali and their justification based on spiritual appearances, as well as the implications that led them to call the imams gods.

Core Idea
The Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya claimed God appeared in Ali's form and spoke through him, thus calling the imams gods.

The Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya are among the extremist sects accused of deifying Ali. This passage presents their argument for incarnation, using analogies from angelic and satanic appearances, and explains why they attribute divinity to Ali and his descendants. Understanding this deviant belief is important for distinguishing orthodox Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall claim.
2
Identify the core belief
Notice that they assert God incarnated in Ali. Find where this is stated.
The phrase 'حل الله في علي' is key.
3
Analyze the justification
Examine the analogy they give: spiritual beings can appear in physical forms. They apply this to God appearing in Ali.
They use examples of Jibril and Satan.
4
Understand the conclusion
Note that they call the imams 'gods' because they believe God appears in them. Also note the comparison between the Prophet and Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النصيرية والإسحاقية : قالوا : حل الله في علي ) فإن ظهور الروحاني في الجسد الجسماني مما لا ينكر إما في جانب الخير فكظهور جبريل بصورة ،البشر، وإما في جانب الشر فكظهور الشيطان في صورة الإنسان قالوا ولما كان علي أولاده أفضل من غيرهم وكانوا مؤيدين بتأييدات متعلقة بباطن الأسرار قلنا ظهر الحق تعالى بصورته ونطق بلسانهم وأخذ بأيديهم ومن هاهنا أطلقنا الآلهة على الأئمة ألا ترى أن النبي قاتل المشركين وعلياً قاتل المنافقين فإن النبي يحكم بالظاهر والله يتولى السرائر )
The Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya: they said: God incarnated in Ali. For the appearance of the spiritual in the physical body is not deniable, either on the side of good, like the appearance of Jibril in the form of a human, or on the side of evil, like the appearance of Satan in human form. They said: Since Ali and his children are better than others and are supported by supports related to the inner secrets, we say: The Truth Most High appeared in his form and spoke with their tongues and took their hands. Hence we called the imams gods. Do you not see that the Prophet fought the polytheists and Ali fought the hypocrites, for the Prophet judges by the apparent and God undertakes the inner secrets?
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage describes the Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya sects, who assert that God incarnated in Ali. They justify incarnation by analogies from spiritual beings appearing in physical forms, such as Jibril or Satan. They claim that because Ali and his descendants are superior and supported by divine secrets, God appeared in their form. Therefore, they call the imams gods. They differentiate the Prophet's role (judging externally) from Ali's role (fighting hypocrites, dealing with inner truths).

Key Terms
النصيرية
الإسحاقية
حل الله
الروحاني
الجسماني
الإلهة على الأئمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Nuṣayriyya only excessively honor the imams but do not actually call them gods.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they call the imams 'gods' (āliha), using the term directly.

Checkpoint
According to the Nuṣayriyya, why can the imams be called gods?
A) Because they are prophets.
B) Because God appeared in them and spoke through them.
C) Because they created the world.
D) Because they are angels.

Key Takeaway
The Nuṣayriyya and Isḥāqiyya sects believe in the incarnation of God in ʿAlī and his descendants, leading them to call the imams gods, which is a clear deviation from Islamic monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Nuṣayriyya justified calling imams gods by appealing to analogies that seemed plausible. Have you ever rationalized a belief or action that you knew was wrong by comparing it to something permissible? What one step will you take today to hold yourself accountable to the Qur'an and Sunnah alone?
?
This sect thought they saw the 'inner secrets' and so elevated Ali beyond his status. Do you ever seek knowledge of the unseen or spiritual rank from other than Allah—through dreams, saints, or speculation? Will you repent and turn back to Allah alone, asking Him for guidance through revelation?
?
If you truly believed that no human—no matter how great—is a god, how would your love for the Prophet's family change? Write down one specific way you will worship Allah alone today, without associating any created being in that worship.
